The goal of this assignment is to learn about the Naive Bayes Classifier (NBC). In this assignment we're going to implement the NBC from scratch without using NBC library in python and perform sentiment analysis using text dataset about amazon reviews. The Naive Bayes classifier is a probabilistic machine learning technique used for classification. The algorithm works based on the assumption that the predictors space are independent. The NBC makes use of the Bayes theorem.


## Load Data

The Amazon review dataset is considered for analysis in this project. This data can be obtained from the following sources: https://www.kaggle.com/marklvl/sentiment-labelled-sentences-data-set. The data is made up of 1000 positive and negative senitments extracted from the reviews of products. A review is considered a positive sentiment if it takes a value of 1 and 0 otherwise.

```python
column_list = ["Reviews", "Sentiment_label"]
dataset = pd.read_csv('amazon_cells_labelled.txt', delimiter = '\t', quoting = 3,names=column_list)
```

## Split data into training and test sets

The dataset is split into training set and testing set. The split ratio was 80% training and 20% testing sets. The training set will be used to train our NBC and the test set will be used for validating our classifier.

## Data cleaning

The original dataset is very messy, containing lots of punctuations, abbreviations, html format, numbers, and upper case letter. We removed all the unwanted information in the data and also change all upper case letters to small case letters. A preview of part of the cleaned data is shown below.

```python
def clean(text):
    no_html = BeautifulSoup(text).get_text()
    clean = re.sub("[^a-z\s]+", " ", no_html, flags=re.IGNORECASE)
    text = text.lower().strip()
    text = " ".join([w for w in text.split() if len(w) > 2])
    text = re.sub('\[.*?\]', '', text)
    text = re.sub('https?://\S+|www\.\S+', '', text)
    text = re.sub('<.*?>+', '', text)
    text = re.sub('[%s]' % re.escape(string.punctuation), '', text)
    text = re.sub('\n', '', text)
    text = re.sub('\w*\d\w*', '', text)
    return re.sub("(\s+)", " ", text)
dataset['Reviews'] = dataset['Reviews'].apply(clean)
print(dataset)
```

## Tokenization

After cleaning our dataset, we proceed to create the Bag of Words model. We do this by splitting the text into individual words process known as tokenization. Words that occur in less than or equal 5 times were removed from the dataset. The output below shows some of the words in the data and the corresponding number of times they appear in the text.

```python
def tokenize(X):                
    freq = {}
    for txt in X:
        tokens = nltk.RegexpTokenizer(r"\w+").tokenize(txt)     
        for token in tokens:                                    
            if token not in freq.keys():
                freq[token] = 1
            else:
                freq[token] += 1
                
    return freq  


def sort_freq(freq_X):                       
    word_sorted = dict(sorted(freq_X.items(), key=lambda x: x[1], reverse=True))
    return word_sorted
    
def remove_rareword(word_sorted):               
    remove = []                                      
    for key, freq in word_sorted.items(): 
        if freq <= 5 : 
            remove.append(key) 

    for i in remove: 
        del word_sorted[i] 
    return   word_sorted
    
```

```python
train_freq = tokenize(X)
sorted_train = sort_freq(train_freq)
sorted_train = remove_rareword(sorted_train)

test_freq = tokenize(X_test)
sorted_test = sort_freq(test_freq)
sorted_test = remove_rareword(sorted_test)


trp_freq = tokenize(X1)
sorted_trp = sort_freq(trp_freq)
sorted_trp = remove_rareword(sorted_trp)

trn_freq = tokenize(X2)
sorted_trn = sort_freq(trn_freq)
sorted_trn = remove_rareword(sorted_trn)


tep_freq = tokenize(X_test1)
sorted_tep = sort_freq(tep_freq)
sorted_tep = remove_rareword(sorted_tep)


ten_freq = tokenize(X_test2)
sorted_ten = sort_freq(ten_freq)
sorted_ten = remove_rareword(sorted_ten)
```

## Creating reverse index matrix

In this section we created a sparse matrix of 0's and 1's for our given data. 

```python
def sparse_matrix_review(X):
    words_vectors = []
    for txt,i  in zip(X,y):   
        words_tokens = nltk.RegexpTokenizer(r"\w+").tokenize(txt)       
        sparse_vec = []
        for token in sorted_train:   
            if token in words_tokens:
                sparse_vec.append(1)
            else:
                sparse_vec.append(0)
        words_vectors.append([sparse_vec,i])
    return words_vectors
```

```python
words_vectors_review = np.asarray(sparse_matrix_review(X))  
print(words_vectors_review[:1]) 
```

## Calculating probabilities

As stateed earlier, NBC uses the Bayes theorem and with a strong assumption that the set of predictors in the data are independent. In this case, we will assume that each word is independent of other words.

The Bayes theorem is defined as; P(Y∣X)= P(X∣Y)P(Y)/P(X), where X and Y are events, P(Y) is the prior probability, P(X∣Y) is the likelihood, and P(X) is the predictor prior probability. This equation is read as the conditional probability of event Y occurring given X has already occurred. 

To find this probability, we will calculate the probability of X occurring given Y has occurred and multiply the results by the probability of Y occurring and then divid everything by the probability of X occurring.

In this section, we calculated the following:

1. The P\[the probability of "the" occurring]; Over here, we take the total number of "the" that occurred in the data and divid this by the total number of words in the data.
2. The P\[the probability of "the" occurring / "positive" review]; Over here, we took the total number of "the" that occurred in the positive data and divide this by the total number of positive words in the data.

```python
count=0

for txt in X:
    tokens = nltk.RegexpTokenizer(r"\w+").tokenize(txt)    
    if 'the' in tokens:
        count+=1
                
count1=0
for txt in X1:
    tokens = nltk.RegexpTokenizer(r"\w+").tokenize(txt)    
    if 'the' in tokens:
        count1+=1
```

```python
print("number of ‘the’", count)   
print ("P[The]= ",count/ len(X))

print()
              
print("number of postive ‘the’", count1)   
print ("P[The| Postive]= ", count1/ len(X1))
     
```

```
number of ‘the’ 244
P[The]=  0.305

number of postive ‘the’ 147
P[The| Postive]=  0.294
```

```
[0.154, 0.16, 0.102, 0.092, 0.088, 0.1, 0.084, 0.068, 0.082, 0.052]
```

```python
Pos_Prob=list()                                   
for i ,j in zip(trp_list,probablity_pos):
    Pos_Prob.append([i,j])
    
Neg_Prob=list()                                   
for i ,j in zip(trn_list,probablity_neg):
    Neg_Prob.append([i,j])
     
```

```python
probablity_pos_class = np.log(sum(probablity_pos))* float(len(X1)/len(X)) 
probablity_neg_class = np.log(sum(probablity_neg))* float(len(X2)/len(X))

print(probablity_pos_class)
print(probablity_pos_class)
```

```
0.709942545882375
0.709942545882375
```

Laplace Smoothing

```python
#Smoothing  Postive
probablity_pos_Laplace = [float(x+1)/float(len(X1)+2) for x in train_sum_pos]  
probablity_pos_class_Laplace = np.log(sum(probablity_pos_Laplace))* float(len(X1)/len(X))

probablity_neg_Laplace = [float(x+1)/float(len(X2)+2) for x in train_sum_neg]  
probablity_neg_class_Laplace = np.log(sum(probablity_neg_Laplace))

print(probablity_neg_class_Laplace)
print(probablity_pos_class_Laplace)
```

```
1.30596110659363
0.7966982057315095
```

Printing words frequencies in training set

```python
freq_post = dict(zip(trp_list,probablity_pos)) 
tokens_without_sw = [word for word in freq_post.keys() if not word in stopwords.words()]

freq_neg = dict(zip(trn_list,probablity_neg)) 
tokens_without_sw_negative = [word for word in freq_neg.keys() if not word in stopwords.words()]

print(tokens_without_sw[:10])

print(tokens_without_sw[1:11])
```

```
['I', 'phone', 'great', 'good', 'The', 'It', 'Great', 'This', 'well', 'works']
['phone', 'great', 'good', 'The', 'It', 'Great', 'This', 'well', 'works', 'quality']
```

```python
def classify(review):
    pos_p = 1
    neg_p = 1
    for word in review:
        if word in Pos_Prob:
            pos_p *=Pos_Prob[word] * float(len(X1)/len(X))
        if word in Neg_Prob:
            neg_p = neg_p[word] * float(len(X2)/len(X))
    if(pos_p>=neg_p):
        return 1
    if(pos_p<neg_p):
        return 0

print(classify('text'))
```

```
1
```

Determining accuracy from the development dataset

```python
predict_label = 0
for row in dev_set[:,1]:
    if row == int(classify('text')):
        predict_label+=1
        
accuracy = float(predict_label/len(dev_set))
print(accuracy)    
```

```
0.48333333333333334
```

Cross-validation

```python
def cross_validation(dataset, k):
    dataset_split = list()
    dataset_copy = list(dataset)
    fold_size = int(len(dataset) / k)     
    for i in range(k):
        fold = list()
        while len(fold) < fold_size:
            index = randrange(len(dataset_copy))
            fold.append(dataset_copy.pop(index))
        dataset_split.append(fold)
    return dataset_split


seed(10)
dataset = dev_set
k = cross_validation(dataset, 5)
print(k)
```

## Final accuracy after cross-validation

Before performing cross-validation, our classifier had predicted accuracy of 48.3%. However, after cross-validation, we obtained an accuracy rate of 51.5%. This suggest an improvement over the initial model.

```python
predict_label__ = 0
for row in words_vectors_review_test[:,1]:
    if row == int(classify('text')):
        predict_label__+=1
        
accuracy__ = float(predict_label__/len(words_vectors_review_test))
print(accuracy__) 
```

```
0.515
```
